</div>
			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Energy storage</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_grid_integration" class="mw-redirect" title="Wind power grid integration">Wind power grid integration</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_energy_storage_projects" title="List of energy storage projects">List of energy storage projects</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stwlan.dam.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Stwlan.dam.jpg/220px-Stwlan.dam.jpg" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Stwlan.dam.jpg/330px-Stwlan.dam.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Stwlan.dam.jpg/440px-Stwlan.dam.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="483" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stwlan.dam.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Llyn Stwlan dam of the <a href="/wiki/Ffestiniog_Power_Station" title="Ffestiniog Power Station">Ffestiniog</a> <a href="/wiki/Pumped-storage_hydroelectricity" title="Pumped-storage hydroelectricity">Pumped Storage Scheme</a> in Wales. The lower power station has four water turbines which can generate a total of 360 MW of electricity for several hours, an example of artificial energy storage and conversion.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Energy storage</b> is the capture of energy produced at one time for use at a later time. A device that stores energy is sometimes called an <a href="/wiki/Accumulator_(energy)" title="Accumulator (energy)">accumulator</a>. Energy comes in multiple forms including radiation, <a href="/wiki/Chemical_energy" title="Chemical energy">chemical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gravitation" class="mw-redirect" title="Gravitation">gravitational potential</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electric_potential_energy" title="Electric potential energy">electrical potential</a>, electricity, elevated temperature, <a href="/wiki/Latent_heat" title="Latent heat">latent heat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic</a>. Energy storage involves converting energy from forms that are difficult to store to more conveniently or economically storable forms. Bulk energy storage is dominated by pumped hydro, which accounts for 99% of global energy storage.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Some technologies provide short-term energy storage, while others can endure for much longer.</p>
<p>A wind-up clock stores potential energy (in this case mechanical, in the spring tension), a <a href="/wiki/Rechargeable_battery" title="Rechargeable battery">rechargeable battery</a> stores readily convertible chemical energy to operate a mobile phone, and a <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectric</a> dam stores <a href="/wiki/Electrical_power_industry" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical power industry">energy</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Reservoir" title="Reservoir">reservoir</a> as gravitational <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">potential energy</a>. <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">Fossil fuels</a> such as coal and gasoline store ancient energy derived from sunlight by organisms that later died, became buried and over time were then converted into these fuels. <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">Food</a> (which is made by the same process as fossil fuels) is a form of energy stored in <a href="/wiki/Chemical" class="mw-redirect" title="Chemical">chemical</a> form.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ice_storage_air_conditioning" title="Ice storage air conditioning">Ice storage</a> tanks store ice frozen by cheaper energy at night to meet peak daytime demand for cooling. The energy isn't stored directly, but the work-product of consuming energy (pumping away heat) is stored, having the equivalent effect on daytime consumption.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Prehistory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The energy present at the initial formation of the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a> is stored in stars such as the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>, and is used by humans directly (e.g. through <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar heating</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sun_tanning" title="Sun tanning">sun tanning</a>), or indirectly (e.g. by growing crops, consuming photosynthesized plants or conversion into electricity in solar cells).</p>
<p>As a purposeful activity, energy storage has existed since pre-history, though it was often not explicitly recognized as such. An example of mechanical energy storage is the use of logs or boulders as defensive measures in ancient forts—the logs or boulders were collected at the top of a hill or wall, and the energy thus stored was used to attack invaders who came within range. Certainly, storing dried wood or another source for fire, or preserving edible food or seeds, in dry or cool areas such as in a cave, under rocks or underground, serves as other examples of energy storage.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_History">Recent History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Recent History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the twentieth century grid electrical power was largely generated by burning fossil fuel. When less power was required, less fuel was burned. Concerns with air pollution, energy imports and global warming have spawned the growth of renewable energy such as solar and wind power. Wind power is uncontrolled and may be generating at a time when no additional power is needed. Solar power varies with cloud cover and at best is only available during daylight hours, while demand often peaks after sunset (<i>see</i> <a href="/wiki/Duck_curve" title="Duck curve">duck curve</a>). Interest in storing power from these intermittent sources grows as the renewable energy industry begins to generate a larger fraction of overall energy consumption.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Off-the-grid" title="Off-the-grid">Off grid electrical</a> use was a niche market in the twentieth century, but in the twenty first century it has expanded. Portable devices are in use all over the world. Solar panels are now a common sight in the rural settings worldwide. Access to electricity is now a question of economics, not location. Powering transportation without burning fuel, however, remains in development.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Methods">Methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Outline">Outline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Outline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Outline_of_energy_storage" title="Outline of energy storage">Outline of energy storage</a></div>
<p>The following list includes natural and other non-commercial types of energy storage. in addition to those designed for use in industry and commerce:</p>
<div class="div-col columns column-count column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; -webkit-column-count: 2; column-count: 2;">
<ul>
<li>Mechanical
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Compressed_air_energy_storage" title="Compressed air energy storage">Compressed air energy storage</a> (CAES)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fireless_locomotive" title="Fireless locomotive">Fireless locomotive</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Flywheel_energy_storage" title="Flywheel energy storage">Flywheel energy storage</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">Gravitational potential energy (device)</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_accumulator" title="Hydraulic accumulator">Hydraulic accumulator</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Liquid_nitrogen" title="Liquid nitrogen">Liquid nitrogen</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pumped-storage_hydroelectricity" title="Pumped-storage hydroelectricity">Pumped-storage hydroelectricity</a></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Electrical
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Capacitor" title="Capacitor">Capacitor</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Superconducting_magnetic_energy_storage" title="Superconducting magnetic energy storage">Superconducting magnetic energy storage</a> (SMES)</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Biological
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">Glycogen</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">Starch</a></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Electrochemical

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanical_storage">Mechanical storage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Mechanical storage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Energy can be stored in water pumped to a higher elevation using <a href="/wiki/Pumped_storage" class="mw-redirect" title="Pumped storage">pumped storage</a> methods and also by moving solid matter to higher locations. Other commercial mechanical methods include <a href="/wiki/Compressed-air_energy_storage" class="mw-redirect" title="Compressed-air energy storage">compressing air</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flywheel_energy_storage" title="Flywheel energy storage">flywheels</a> that convert electric energy into kinetic energy and then back again when electrical demand peaks.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydroelectricity">Hydroelectricity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Hydroelectricity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">Hydroelectricity</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">Hydroelectric dams</a> with reservoirs can be operated to provide peak generation at times of peak demand. Water is stored in the reservoir during periods of low demand and released when demand is high. The net effect is similar to pumped storage, but without the pumping loss.</p>
<p>While a hydroelectric dam does not directly store energy from other generating units, it behaves equivalently by lowering output in periods of excess electricity from other sources. In this mode, dams are one of the most efficient forms of energy storage, because only the timing of its generation changes. Hydroelectric turbines have a start-up time on the order of a few minutes.<sup id="cite_ref-Huggins2010_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Huggins2010-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pumped-storage">Pumped-storage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Pumped-storage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Adam_Beck_Complex.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Adam_Beck_Complex.jpg/220px-Adam_Beck_Complex.jpg" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Adam_Beck_Complex.jpg/330px-Adam_Beck_Complex.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Adam_Beck_Complex.jpg/440px-Adam_Beck_Complex.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="719" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Adam_Beck_Complex.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Sir_Adam_Beck_Hydroelectric_Generating_Stations" title="Sir Adam Beck Hydroelectric Generating Stations">Sir Adam Beck Generating Complex</a> at <a href="/wiki/Niagara_Falls,_Ontario" title="Niagara Falls, Ontario">Niagara Falls, Canada</a>, which includes a large <a href="/wiki/Pumped-storage_hydroelectricity" title="Pumped-storage hydroelectricity">pumped storage hydroelectricity reservoir</a> to provide an extra 174 MW of electricity during periods of peak demand.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pumped-storage_hydroelectricity" title="Pumped-storage hydroelectricity">Pumped-storage hydroelectricity</a></div>
<p>Worldwide, pumped-storage <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectricity</a> (PSH) is the largest-capacity form of active <a href="/wiki/Grid_energy_storage" title="Grid energy storage">grid energy storage</a> available, and, as of March 2012, the <a href="/wiki/Electric_Power_Research_Institute" title="Electric Power Research Institute">Electric Power Research Institute</a> (EPRI) reports that PSH accounts for more than 99% of bulk storage capacity worldwide, representing around 127,000 <a href="/wiki/Megawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Megawatt">MW</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EconomistPSH_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EconomistPSH-2">[2]</a></sup> PSH reported <a href="/wiki/Energy_conversion_efficiency" title="Energy conversion efficiency">energy efficiency</a> varies in practice between 70% and 80%,<sup id="cite_ref-EconomistPSH_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EconomistPSH-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-thier_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thier-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Levine_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levine-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-yang_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yang-5">[5]</a></sup> with claims of up to 87%.<sup id="cite_ref-heco_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-heco-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>At times of low electrical demand, excess generation capacity is used to pump water from a lower source into a higher reservoir. When demand grows, water is released back into a lower reservoir (or waterway or body of water) through a <a href="/wiki/Turbine" title="Turbine">turbine</a>, generating electricity. Reversible turbine-generator assemblies act as both a pump and turbine (usually a <a href="/wiki/Francis_turbine" title="Francis turbine">Francis turbine</a> design). Nearly all facilities use the height difference between two water bodies. Pure pumped-storage plants shift the water between reservoirs, while the "pump-back" approach is a combination of pumped storage and conventional <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectric plants</a> that use natural stream-flow.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Compressed_air">Compressed air</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Compressed air">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Compressed_Air_Loco.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Compressed_Air_Loco.jpg/220px-Compressed_Air_Loco.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Compressed_Air_Loco.jpg/330px-Compressed_Air_Loco.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Compressed_Air_Loco.jpg/440px-Compressed_Air_Loco.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Compressed_Air_Loco.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Fireless_locomotive" title="Fireless locomotive">compressed air locomotive</a> used inside a mine between 1928 and 1961.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Compressed_air_energy_storage" title="Compressed air energy storage">Compressed air energy storage</a></div>
<p>Compressed air energy storage (CAES) uses surplus energy to compress air for subsequent electricity generation.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-2010.07.28_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-2010.07.28-7">[7]</a></sup> Small scale systems have long been used in such applications as propulsion of mine locomotives. The compressed air is stored in an underground reservoir.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gas_compressor" title="Gas compressor">Compression</a> of <a href="/wiki/Air" class="mw-redirect" title="Air">air</a> creates heat; the air is warmer after compression. <a href="/wiki/Thermal_expansion" title="Thermal expansion">Expansion</a> requires heat. If no extra heat is added, the air will be much colder after expansion. If the heat generated during compression can be stored and used during expansion, efficiency improves considerably.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2012.10.01_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2012.10.01-8">[8]</a></sup> A CAES system can deal with the heat in three ways. Air storage can be <a href="/wiki/Adiabatic" class="mw-redirect" title="Adiabatic">adiabatic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diabatic" title="Diabatic">diabatic</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Isothermal" class="mw-redirect" title="Isothermal">isothermal</a>. Another approach uses compressed air to power vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-Auto.com-2004.03.18_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Auto.com-2004.03.18-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Freep-2004.03.18_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Freep-2004.03.18-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Flywheel_energy_storage">Flywheel energy storage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Flywheel energy storage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Example_of_cylindrical_flywheel_rotor_assembly.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Example_of_cylindrical_flywheel_rotor_assembly.png/220px-Example_of_cylindrical_flywheel_rotor_assembly.png" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Example_of_cylindrical_flywheel_rotor_assembly.png/330px-Example_of_cylindrical_flywheel_rotor_assembly.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Example_of_cylindrical_flywheel_rotor_assembly.png/440px-Example_of_cylindrical_flywheel_rotor_assembly.png 2x" data-file-width="1961" data-file-height="1456" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Example_of_cylindrical_flywheel_rotor_assembly.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The main components of a typical flywheel.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Flywheel_energy_storage" title="Flywheel energy storage">Flywheel energy storage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flywheel_storage_power_system" title="Flywheel storage power system">Flywheel storage power system</a></div>
<p>Flywheel energy storage (FES) works by accelerating a rotor (<a href="/wiki/Flywheel" title="Flywheel">flywheel</a>) to a very high speed, holding energy as <a href="/wiki/Rotational_energy" title="Rotational energy">rotational energy</a>. When energy is extracted, the flywheel's rotational speed declines as a consequence of <a href="/wiki/Conservation_of_energy" title="Conservation of energy">conservation of energy</a>; adding energy correspondingly results in an increase in the speed of the flywheel.</p>
<p>Most FES systems use electricity to accelerate and decelerate the flywheel, but devices that directly use mechanical energy are under consideration.<sup id="cite_ref-Torotrak_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Torotrak-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>FES systems have rotors made of high strength <a href="/wiki/Carbon-fiber" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon-fiber">carbon-fiber</a> composites, suspended by <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_bearing" title="Magnetic bearing">magnetic bearings</a> and spinning at speeds from 20,000 to over 50,000&#160;rpm in a vacuum enclosure.<sup id="cite_ref-ScienceNews_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceNews-12">[12]</a></sup> Such flywheels can reach maximum speed&#160;("charge") in a matter of minutes. The flywheel system is connected to a combination <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">electric motor</a>/<a href="/wiki/Electric_generator" title="Electric generator">generator</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flybrid_Systems_Kinetic_Energy_Recovery_System.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Flybrid_Systems_Kinetic_Energy_Recovery_System.jpg/220px-Flybrid_Systems_Kinetic_Energy_Recovery_System.jpg" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Flybrid_Systems_Kinetic_Energy_Recovery_System.jpg/330px-Flybrid_Systems_Kinetic_Energy_Recovery_System.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Flybrid_Systems_Kinetic_Energy_Recovery_System.jpg/440px-Flybrid_Systems_Kinetic_Energy_Recovery_System.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1540" data-file-height="1150" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flybrid_Systems_Kinetic_Energy_Recovery_System.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Flybrid <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_Energy_Recovery_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Kinetic Energy Recovery System">Kinetic Energy Recovery System</a> <a href="/wiki/Flywheel_energy_storage" title="Flywheel energy storage">flywheel</a>. Built for use on <a href="/wiki/Formula_One" title="Formula One">Formula 1 racing cars</a>, it is employed to recover and reuse kinetic energy captured during braking.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>FES systems have relatively long lifetimes (lasting decades with little or no maintenance;<sup id="cite_ref-ScienceNews_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceNews-12">[12]</a></sup> full-cycle lifetimes quoted for flywheels range from in excess of 10<sup>5</sup>, up to 10<sup>7</sup>, cycles of use),<sup id="cite_ref-Investire_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Investire-13">[13]</a></sup> high <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> (100–130&#160;W·h/kg, or 360–500 kJ/kg)<sup id="cite_ref-Investire_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Investire-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pddnet_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pddnet-14">[14]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Power_density" title="Power density">power density</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gravitational_potential_energy_storage_with_solid_masses">Gravitational potential energy storage with solid masses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Gravitational potential energy storage with solid masses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Changing the altitude of solid masses can store or release energy via an elevating system driven by an electric motor/generator.</p>
<p>Companies such as Energy Cache and Advanced Rail Energy Storage (ARES) are working on this.<sup id="cite_ref-EnergyCache.com_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EnergyCache.com-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Scientific_American-2014.03.25_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scientific_American-2014.03.25-16">[16]</a></sup> ARES uses rails to move concrete weights up and down.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Stratosolar proposes to use winches supported by buoyant platforms at an altitude of 20 kilometers, to raise and lower solid masses.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Sink Float Solutions proposes to use winches supported by an ocean barge for taking advantage of a 4&#160;km (13,000&#160;ft) elevation difference between the surface and the seabed.<sup id="cite_ref-sinkfloatsolutions.com_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sinkfloatsolutions.com-19">[19]</a></sup> ARES estimated a capital cost for the storage capacity of around 60% of pump storage hydroelectricity,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Stratosolar $100/kWh<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> and Sink Float Solutions $25/kWh (4000&#160;m depth) and $50/kWh (with 2000&#160;m depth).<sup id="cite_ref-sinkfloatsolutions.com_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sinkfloatsolutions.com-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Potential energy storage or gravity energy storage was under active development in 2013 in association with the <a href="/wiki/California_Independent_System_Operator" title="California Independent System Operator">California Independent System Operator</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Economist-2012.03.03_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist-2012.03.03-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bloomberg-2012.09.06_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomberg-2012.09.06-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kernan_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kernan-24">[24]</a></sup> It examined the movement of earth-filled <a href="/wiki/Hopper_car" title="Hopper car">hopper rail cars</a> driven by <a href="/wiki/Electric_locomotive" title="Electric locomotive">electric locomotives</a>) from lower to higher elevations.<sup id="cite_ref-Scientific_American-2014.03.25_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scientific_American-2014.03.25-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>ARES claimed advantages including indefinite storage with no energy losses, low costs when earth/rocks are used and conservation of water resources.<sup id="cite_ref-Scientific_American-2014.03.25_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scientific_American-2014.03.25-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BlackleMag_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BlackleMag-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermal_storage">Thermal storage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Thermal storage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fernw%C3%A4rmespricher_Theiss.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Fernw%C3%A4rmespricher_Theiss.JPG/220px-Fernw%C3%A4rmespricher_Theiss.JPG" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Fernw%C3%A4rmespricher_Theiss.JPG/330px-Fernw%C3%A4rmespricher_Theiss.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Fernw%C3%A4rmespricher_Theiss.JPG/440px-Fernw%C3%A4rmespricher_Theiss.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1969" data-file-height="1447" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fernw%C3%A4rmespricher_Theiss.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
District heating accumulation tower from Theiss near <a href="/wiki/Krems_an_der_Donau" title="Krems an der Donau">Krems an der Donau</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lower_Austria" title="Lower Austria">Lower Austria</a> with a thermal capacity of 2 GWh</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy_storage" title="Thermal energy storage">Thermal energy storage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seasonal_thermal_energy_storage" title="Seasonal thermal energy storage">Seasonal thermal energy storage</a></div>
<p>Thermal storage is the temporary storage or removal of heat. TES is practical because of water's large <a href="/wiki/Heat_of_fusion" class="mw-redirect" title="Heat of fusion">heat of fusion</a>: the melting of one <a href="/wiki/Metric_ton" class="mw-redirect" title="Metric ton">metric ton</a> of ice (approximately one cubic metre in size) can capture 334 <a href="/wiki/Megajoule" class="mw-redirect" title="Megajoule">megajoules</a> [MJ] (317,000&#160;<a href="/wiki/British_thermal_unit" title="British thermal unit">BTU</a>) of thermal energy.</p>
<p>An example is Alberta, Canada's <a href="/wiki/Drake_Landing_Solar_Community" title="Drake Landing Solar Community">Drake Landing Solar Community</a><i>,</i> for which 97% of the year-round heat is provided by solar-thermal collectors on the garage roofs, with a borehole thermal energy store (BTES) being the enabling technology.<sup id="cite_ref-Wong_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wong-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DistrictEnergy.org-a_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DistrictEnergy.org-a-27">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> STES projects often have paybacks in the four-to-six year range.<sup id="cite_ref-Hellstr.C3.B6m_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hellstr.C3.B6m-29">[29]</a></sup> In Braestrup, Denmark, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Denmark" title="Solar power in Denmark">the community's solar district heating system</a> also utilizes STES, at a storage temperature of 65&#160;°C (149&#160;°F). A heat pump, which is run only when there is surplus wind power available on the national grid, is used to raise the temperature to 80&#160;°C (176&#160;°F) for distribution. When surplus wind generated electricity is not available, a gas-fired boiler is used. Twenty percent of Braestrup's heat is solar.<sup id="cite_ref-Solar_District_Heating_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Solar_District_Heating-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Latent_heat_thermal_energy_storage_.28LHTES.29"><span id="Latent_heat_thermal_energy_storage"></span> Latent heat thermal energy storage (LHTES)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Latent heat thermal energy storage (LHTES)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Latent heat thermal energy storage systems works with materials with high latent heat (heat of fusion) capacity, known as <a href="/wiki/Phase-change_material" title="Phase-change material">phase change materials</a> (PCMs). The main advantage of these materials is that their latent heat storage capacity is much more than sensible heat. In a specific temperature range, phase changes from solid to liquid absorbs a large amount of thermal energy for later use.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electrochemical">Electrochemical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Electrochemical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rechargeable_battery">Rechargeable battery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Rechargeable battery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Datacenter_Backup_Batteries.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Datacenter_Backup_Batteries.jpg/220px-Datacenter_Backup_Batteries.jpg" width="220" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Datacenter_Backup_Batteries.jpg/330px-Datacenter_Backup_Batteries.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Datacenter_Backup_Batteries.jpg/440px-Datacenter_Backup_Batteries.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2124" data-file-height="2118" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Datacenter_Backup_Batteries.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A rechargeable battery bank used as an <a href="/wiki/Uninterruptible_power_supply" title="Uninterruptible power supply">uninterruptible power supply</a> in a data center</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Rechargeable_battery" title="Rechargeable battery">Rechargeable battery</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battery_storage_power_station" title="Battery storage power station">Battery storage power station</a></div>
<p>A rechargeable battery, comprises one or more <a href="/wiki/Electrochemical_cell" title="Electrochemical cell">electrochemical cells</a>. It is known as a 'secondary cell' because its <a href="/wiki/Electrochemistry" title="Electrochemistry">electrochemical</a> <a href="/wiki/Chemical_reaction" title="Chemical reaction">reactions</a> are electrically reversible. Rechargeable batteries come in many different shapes and sizes, ranging from <a href="/wiki/Button_cell#Rechargeable_variants" title="Button cell">button cells</a> to megawatt grid systems.</p>
<p>Rechargeable batteries have lower total cost of use and environmental impact than non-rechargeable (disposable) batteries. Some rechargeable battery types are available in the same <a href="/wiki/List_of_battery_sizes" title="List of battery sizes">form factors</a> as disposables. Rechargeable batteries have higher initial cost but can be recharged very cheaply and used many times.</p>
<p>Common rechargeable battery chemistries include:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lead%E2%80%93acid_battery" title="Lead–acid battery">Lead–acid battery</a>: Lead acid batteries hold the largest market share of electric storage products. A single cell produces about 2V when charged. In the charged state the metallic lead negative electrode and the <a href="/wiki/Lead_sulfate" class="mw-redirect" title="Lead sulfate">lead sulfate</a> positive electrode are immersed in a dilute <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> (H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>) <a href="/wiki/Electrolyte" title="Electrolyte">electrolyte</a>. In the discharge process electrons are pushed out of the cell as lead sulfate is formed at the negative electrode while the electrolyte is reduced to water.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nickel%E2%80%93cadmium_battery" title="Nickel–cadmium battery">Nickel–cadmium battery</a> (NiCd): Uses <a href="/wiki/Nickel_oxide_hydroxide" title="Nickel oxide hydroxide">nickel oxide hydroxide</a> and metallic <a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">cadmium</a> as <a href="/wiki/Electrode" title="Electrode">electrodes</a>. Cadmium is a toxic element, and was banned for most uses by the European Union in 2004. Nickel–cadmium batteries have been almost completely replaced by nickel–metal hydride (NiMH) batteries.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nickel%E2%80%93metal_hydride_battery" title="Nickel–metal hydride battery">Nickel–metal hydride battery</a> (NiMH): First commercial types were available in 1989.<sup id="cite_ref-Aifantis_et_al_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aifantis_et_al-31">[31]</a></sup> These are now a common consumer and industrial type. The battery has a hydrogen-absorbing <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloy</a> for the negative <a href="/wiki/Electrode" title="Electrode">electrode</a> instead of <a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">cadmium</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lithium-ion_battery" title="Lithium-ion battery">Lithium-ion battery</a>: The choice in many consumer electronics and have one of the best <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy-to-mass ratios</a> and a very slow self-dis<a href="/wiki/Loss_of_charge" class="mw-redirect" title="Loss of charge">charge</a> when not in use.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lithium-ion_polymer_battery" class="mw-redirect" title="Lithium-ion polymer battery">Lithium-ion polymer battery</a>: These batteries are light in weight and can be made in any shape desired.</li>
</ul>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Flow_battery">Flow battery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Flow battery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Flow_battery" title="Flow battery">Flow battery</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vanadium_redox_battery" title="Vanadium redox battery">Vanadium redox battery</a></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Flow_battery" title="Flow battery">flow battery</a> operates by passing a solution over a membrane where ions are exchanged to charge/discharge the cell. <a href="/wiki/Electrode_potential#Potential_difference_of_a_cell_assembled_of_two_electrodes" title="Electrode potential">Cell voltage</a> is chemically determined by the <a href="/wiki/Nernst_equation" title="Nernst equation">Nernst equation</a> and ranges, in practical applications, from 1.0 to 2.2 V. Its storage capacity is a function of the volume of the tanks holding the solution.</p>
<p>A flow battery is technically akin both to a <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell" title="Fuel cell">fuel cell</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Electrochemical_cell" title="Electrochemical cell">electrochemical accumulator cell</a>. Commercial applications are for long half-cycle storage such as backup grid power.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Supercapacitor">Supercapacitor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Supercapacitor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Expo_2010_Electric_Bus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Expo_2010_Electric_Bus.jpg/220px-Expo_2010_Electric_Bus.jpg" width="220" height="126" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Expo_2010_Electric_Bus.jpg/330px-Expo_2010_Electric_Bus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Expo_2010_Electric_Bus.jpg/440px-Expo_2010_Electric_Bus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="910" data-file-height="520" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Expo_2010_Electric_Bus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
One of a fleet of <a href="/wiki/Capa_vehicle" title="Capa vehicle">electric capabuses</a> powered by supercapacitors, at a quick-charge station-bus stop, in service during <a href="/wiki/Expo_2010" title="Expo 2010">Expo 2010 Shanghai China</a>. Charging rails can be seen suspended over the bus.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Supercapacitor" title="Supercapacitor">Supercapacitor</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Supercapacitor" title="Supercapacitor">Supercapacitors</a>, also called electric double-layer capacitors (EDLC) or ultracapacitors, are generic terms for a family of <a href="/wiki/Electrochemistry" title="Electrochemistry">electrochemical</a> <a href="/wiki/Capacitor" title="Capacitor">capacitors</a><sup id="cite_ref-Conway_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conway-32">[32]</a></sup> that do not have conventional solid <a href="/wiki/Dielectric" title="Dielectric">dielectrics</a>. <a href="/wiki/Capacitance" title="Capacitance">Capacitance</a> is determined by two storage principles, double-layer capacitance and <a href="/wiki/Pseudocapacitance" title="Pseudocapacitance">pseudocapacitance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Halper_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halper-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Frackowiak1_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frackowiak1-34">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Volfkovich_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Volfkovich-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Supercapacitors bridge the gap between conventional capacitors and <a href="/wiki/Rechargeable_battery" title="Rechargeable battery">rechargeable batteries</a>. They store the most energy per unit volume or mass (<a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a>) among capacitors. They support up to 10,000 <a href="/wiki/Farads" class="mw-redirect" title="Farads">farads</a>/1.2 volt,<sup id="cite_ref-Elton_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elton-36">[36]</a></sup> up to 10,000 times that of <a href="/wiki/Electrolytic_capacitor" title="Electrolytic capacitor">electrolytic capacitors</a>, but deliver or accept less than half as much power per unit time (<a href="/wiki/Power_density" title="Power density">power density</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Conway_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conway-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>While supercapacitors have energy densities that are approximately 10% of batteries, their power density is generally 10 to 100 times greater. This results in much shorter charge/discharge cycles. Additionally, they will tolerate many more charge and discharge cycles than batteries.</p>
<p>Supercapacitors support a broad spectrum of applications, including:</p>
<ul>
<li>Low supply current for memory backup in <a href="/wiki/Static_random-access_memory" title="Static random-access memory">static random-access memory</a> (SRAM)</li>
<li>Power for cars, buses, trains, cranes and elevators, including energy recovery from braking, short-term energy storage and burst-mode power delivery</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="UltraBattery">UltraBattery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: UltraBattery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/UltraBattery" title="UltraBattery">UltraBattery</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/UltraBattery" title="UltraBattery">UltraBattery</a> is a hybrid lead-acid cell and carbon-based <a href="/wiki/Ultracapacitor" class="mw-redirect" title="Ultracapacitor">ultracapacitor</a> (or supercapacitor) invented by Australia’s national research body, the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Scientific_and_Industrial_Research_Organisation" title="Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation">Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation</a> (CSIRO). The lead-acid cell and ultracapacitor share the sulfuric acid electrolyte and both are packaged into the same physical unit.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The UltraBattery can be manufactured with similar physical and electrical characteristics to conventional lead-acid batteries making it possible to cost-effectively replace many lead-acid applications.</p>
<p>The UltraBattery tolerates high charge and discharge levels and endures large numbers of cycles, outperforming previous lead-acid cells by more than an order of magnitude.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> In hybrid-electric vehicle tests, millions of cycles have been achieved.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The UltraBattery is also highly tolerant to the effects of sulfation compared with traditional lead-acid cells.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> This means it can operate continuously in partial state of charge whereas traditional lead-acid batteries are generally held at full charge between discharge events. It is generally electrically inefficient to fully charge a lead-acid battery so by decreasing time spent in the top region of charge the UltraBattery achieves high efficiencies, typically between 85 and 95% DC-DC.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>The UltraBattery can work across a wide range of applications. The constant cycling and fast charging and discharging necessary for applications such as grid regulation and leveling and electric vehicles can damage chemical batteries, but are well handled by the ultracapacitive qualities of UltraBattery technology. The technology has been installed in Australia and the US on the megawatt scale, performing frequency regulation and renewable smoothing applications.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_chemical">Other chemical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Other chemical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Power_to_gas">Power to gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Power to gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Power_to_gas" title="Power to gas">Power to gas</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Power_to_gas" title="Power to gas">Power to gas</a> is a technology which converts <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a> into a gaseous <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> or <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>. The three commercial methods use electricity to reduce <a href="/wiki/Water_splitting" title="Water splitting">water</a> into <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> by means of <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a>.</p>
<p>In the first method, hydrogen is injected into the natural gas grid or is used in transport or industry. The second method is to combine the hydrogen with <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> using a <a href="/wiki/Methanation" title="Methanation">methanation</a> reaction such as the <a href="/wiki/Sabatier_reaction" title="Sabatier reaction">Sabatier reaction</a>, or biological methanation, resulting in an extra energy conversion loss of 8%. The methane may then be fed into the natural gas grid. The third method uses the output gas of a <a href="/wiki/Wood_gas_generator" title="Wood gas generator">wood gas generator</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a> plant, after the <a href="/wiki/Biogas_upgrader" title="Biogas upgrader">biogas upgrader</a> is mixed with the hydrogen from the electrolyzer, to upgrade the quality of the biogas.</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrogen">Hydrogen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Hydrogen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_storage" title="Hydrogen storage">Hydrogen storage</a></div>
<p>The element <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> can be a form of stored energy. Hydrogen can produce electricity via a <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fuel_cell" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrogen fuel cell">hydrogen fuel cell</a>.</p>
<p>At penetrations below 20% of the grid demand, renewables do not severely change the economics; but beyond about 20% of the total demand,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Couldn't find statement in source. (February 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> external storage becomes important.<sup id="cite_ref-Storage_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Storage-42">[42]</a></sup> If these sources are used to make ionic hydrogen, they can be freely expanded. A 5-year community-based pilot program using <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">wind turbines</a> and hydrogen generators began in 2007 in the remote community of <a href="/wiki/Ramea,_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" class="mw-redirect" title="Ramea, Newfoundland and Labrador">Ramea, Newfoundland and Labrador</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NaturalResourcesCan_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NaturalResourcesCan-43">[43]</a></sup> A similar project began in 2004 on <a href="/wiki/Utsira" title="Utsira">Utsira</a>, a small Norwegian island.</p>
<p>Energy losses involved in the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_storage" title="Hydrogen storage">hydrogen storage</a> cycle come from the <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis_of_water" title="Electrolysis of water">electrolysis of water</a>, liquification or compression of the hydrogen and conversion to electricity.<sup id="cite_ref-PhysOrg.com-news-85074285_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PhysOrg.com-news-85074285-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>About 50&#160;kW·h (180 MJ) of solar energy is required to produce a kilogram of hydrogen, so the cost of the electricity is crucial. At $0.03/kWh, a common off-peak high-voltage line rate in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, hydrogen costs $1.50 a kilogram for the electricity, equivalent to $1.50/gallon for <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a>. Other costs include the <a href="/wiki/High_pressure_electrolysis" class="mw-redirect" title="High pressure electrolysis">electrolyzer plant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_compressor" title="Hydrogen compressor">hydrogen compressors</a> or <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">liquefaction</a>, storage and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_infrastructure" title="Hydrogen infrastructure">transportation</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Underground_hydrogen_storage" title="Underground hydrogen storage">Underground hydrogen storage</a> is the practice of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_storage" title="Hydrogen storage">hydrogen storage</a> in underground <a href="/wiki/Cave" title="Cave">caverns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salt_dome" title="Salt dome">salt domes</a> and depleted oil and gas fields.<sup id="cite_ref-Royal_Society_of_Chemistry-a_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Royal_Society_of_Chemistry-a-45">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hyunder_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hyunder-46">[46]</a></sup> Large quantities of gaseous hydrogen have been stored in underground caverns by <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Chemical_Industries" title="Imperial Chemical Industries">Imperial Chemical Industries</a> for many years without any difficulties.<sup id="cite_ref-Hyweb.de_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hyweb.de-47">[47]</a></sup> The European Hyunder project indicated in 2013 that storage of wind and solar energy using underground hydrogen would require 85 caverns.<sup id="cite_ref-Hyunder.eu-b_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hyunder.eu-b-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Methane">Methane</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Methane">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Substitute_natural_gas" title="Substitute natural gas">Substitute natural gas</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">Methane</a> is the simplest hydrocarbon with the molecular formula CH<sub>4</sub>. Methane is more easily stored and transported than hydrogen. Storage and combustion infrastructure (pipelines, <a href="/wiki/Gas_holder" title="Gas holder">gasometers</a>, power plants) are mature.</p>
<p>Synthetic natural gas (<a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">syngas</a> or SNG) can be created in a multi-step process, starting with hydrogen and oxygen. Hydrogen is then reacted with <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Sabatier_reaction" title="Sabatier reaction">Sabatier process</a>, producing methane and water. Methane can be stored and later used to produce electricity. The resulting water is recycled, reducing the need for water. In the electrolysis stage oxygen is stored for methane combustion in a pure oxygen environment at an adjacent power plant, eliminating <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_oxide" title="Nitrogen oxide">nitrogen oxides</a>.</p>
<p>Methane combustion produces carbon dioxide (CO<sub>2</sub>) and water. The carbon dioxide can be recycled to boost the Sabatier process and water can be recycled for further electrolysis. Methane production, storage and combustion recycles the reaction products.</p>
<p>The CO<sub>2</sub> has economic value as a component of an energy storage vector, not a cost as in <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">carbon capture and storage</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Power_to_liquid">Power to liquid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Power to liquid">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Power to liquid is similar to power to gas, however the hydrogen produced by electrolysis from wind and solar electricity isn't converted into gases such as methane but into liquids such as <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>. Methanol is easier in handling than gases and requires less safety precautions than hydrogen. It can be used for <a href="/wiki/Transportation" class="mw-redirect" title="Transportation">transportation</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Aircraft" title="Aircraft">aircraft</a>, but also for industrial purposes or in the power sector.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Biofuels">Biofuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Biofuels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">Biofuel</a></div>
<p>Various <a href="/wiki/Biofuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Biofuels">biofuels</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Straight_vegetable_oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Straight vegetable oil">vegetable oil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_fuel" title="Alcohol fuel">alcohol fuels</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> can replace <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a>. Various chemical processes can convert the carbon and hydrogen in coal, natural gas, plant and animal <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> and organic wastes into short hydrocarbons suitable as replacements for existing hydrocarbon fuels. Examples are <a href="/wiki/Fischer%E2%80%93Tropsch_process" title="Fischer–Tropsch process">Fischer–Tropsch</a> diesel, <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dimethyl_ether" title="Dimethyl ether">dimethyl ether</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">syngas</a>. This diesel source was used extensively in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> in Germany, which faced limited access to crude oil supplies. South Africa produces most of the country's diesel from coal for similar reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-EPA-2002.a_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPA-2002.a-50">[50]</a></sup> A long term oil price above US$35/bbl may make such large scale synthetic liquid fuels economical.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aluminium.2C_boron.2C_silicon.2C_and_zinc">Aluminium, boron, silicon, and zinc</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Aluminium, boron, silicon, and zinc">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">Aluminium</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Alchemy_white_paper_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alchemy_white_paper-51">[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">Boron</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Cowan_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cowan-52">[52]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Auner_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Auner-53">[53]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a><sup id="cite_ref-Engineer-Poet_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Engineer-Poet-54">[54]</a></sup> have been proposed as energy storage solutions.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electrical_methods">Electrical methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Electrical methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Capacitor">Capacitor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Capacitor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Capacitor" title="Capacitor">capacitor</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mylar-film_oil-filled_low-inductance_capacitor_6.5_MFD_@_5000_VDC.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Mylar-film_oil-filled_low-inductance_capacitor_6.5_MFD_%40_5000_VDC.jpg/175px-Mylar-film_oil-filled_low-inductance_capacitor_6.5_MFD_%40_5000_VDC.jpg" width="175" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Mylar-film_oil-filled_low-inductance_capacitor_6.5_MFD_%40_5000_VDC.jpg/263px-Mylar-film_oil-filled_low-inductance_capacitor_6.5_MFD_%40_5000_VDC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Mylar-film_oil-filled_low-inductance_capacitor_6.5_MFD_%40_5000_VDC.jpg/350px-Mylar-film_oil-filled_low-inductance_capacitor_6.5_MFD_%40_5000_VDC.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2548" data-file-height="3396" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mylar-film_oil-filled_low-inductance_capacitor_6.5_MFD_@_5000_VDC.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
This mylar-film, oil-filled capacitor has very low inductance and low resistance, to provide the high-power (70 megawatts) and the very high speed (1.2 microsecond) discharges needed to operate a <a href="/wiki/Dye_laser" title="Dye laser">dye laser</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Capacitor" title="Capacitor">capacitor</a> (originally known as a 'condenser') is a <a href="/wiki/Passivity_(engineering)" title="Passivity (engineering)">passive</a> <a href="/wiki/Terminal_(electronics)" title="Terminal (electronics)">two-terminal</a> <a href="/wiki/Electronic_component" title="Electronic component">electrical component</a> used to store <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> <a href="/wiki/Electrostatic" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrostatic">electrostatically</a>. Practical capacitors vary widely, but all contain at least two <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">electrical conductors</a> (plates) separated by a <a href="/wiki/Dielectric" title="Dielectric">dielectric</a> (i.e., <a href="/wiki/Insulator_(electricity)" title="Insulator (electricity)">insulator</a>). A capacitor can store electric energy when disconnected from its charging circuit, so it can be used like a temporary <a href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" title="Battery (electricity)">battery</a>, or like other types of <a href="/wiki/Rechargeable_energy_storage_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Rechargeable energy storage system">rechargeable energy storage system</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Miller_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-55">[55]</a></sup> Capacitors are commonly used in electronic devices to maintain power supply while batteries change. (This prevents loss of information in volatile memory.) Conventional capacitors provide less than 360 <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">joules</a> per kilogram, while a conventional <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_battery" title="Alkaline battery">alkaline battery</a> has a density of 590 kJ/kg.</p>
<p>Capacitors store <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> in an <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electrostatic field</a> between their plates. Given a <a href="/wiki/Potential_difference" class="mw-redirect" title="Potential difference">potential difference</a> across the conductors (e.g., when a capacitor is attached across a battery), an <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric field</a> develops across the dielectric, causing positive charge (+Q) to collect on one plate and negative charge (-Q) to collect on the other plate. If a battery is attached to a capacitor for a sufficient amount of time, no current can flow through the capacitor. However, if an accelerating or alternating voltage is applied across the leads of the capacitor, a <a href="/wiki/Displacement_current" title="Displacement current">displacement current</a> can flow.</p>
<p>Capacitance is greater given a narrower separation between conductors and when the conductors have a larger surface area. In practice, the dielectric between the plates emits a small amount of <a href="/wiki/Leakage_(electronics)" title="Leakage (electronics)">leakage current</a> and has an electric field strength limit, known as the <a href="/wiki/Breakdown_voltage" title="Breakdown voltage">breakdown voltage</a>. The conductors and <a href="/wiki/Lead_(electronics)" title="Lead (electronics)">leads</a> introduce undesired <a href="/wiki/Equivalent_series_inductance" title="Equivalent series inductance">inductance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Equivalent_series_resistance" title="Equivalent series resistance">resistance</a>.</p>
<p>Research is assessing the quantum effects of <a href="/wiki/Nanoscopic_scale" title="Nanoscopic scale">nanoscale</a> capacitors for digital quantum batteries.<sup id="cite_ref-TechReview-2009.12.21_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TechReview-2009.12.21-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Complexity-Vol14.Iss3_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Complexity-Vol14.Iss3-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Superconducting_magnetics">Superconducting magnetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Superconducting magnetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Superconducting_magnetic_energy_storage" title="Superconducting magnetic energy storage">Superconducting magnetic energy storage</a></div>
<p>Superconducting magnetic energy storage (SMES) systems store energy in a <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> created by the flow of <a href="/wiki/Direct_current" title="Direct current">direct current</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Superconductivity" title="Superconductivity">superconducting</a> coil that has been cooled to a temperature below its <a href="/wiki/Superconductivity#Superconducting_phase_transition" title="Superconductivity">superconducting critical temperature</a>. A typical SMES system includes a superconducting <a href="/wiki/Inductor" title="Inductor">coil</a>, power conditioning system and refrigerator. Once the superconducting coil is charged, the current does not decay and the magnetic energy can be stored indefinitely.<sup id="cite_ref-Hassenzahl_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hassenzahl-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>The stored energy can be released to the network by discharging the coil. The associated inverter/rectifier accounts for about 2–3% energy loss in each direction. SMES loses the least amount of <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a> in the energy storage process compared to other methods of storing energy. SMES systems offer round-trip efficiency greater than 95%.<sup id="cite_ref-Cheung-Cheung-De_Silvia-Juvonen-Singh-Woo_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cheung-Cheung-De_Silvia-Juvonen-Singh-Woo-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Due to the energy requirements of refrigeration and the cost of <a href="/wiki/Superconducting_wire" title="Superconducting wire">superconducting wire</a>, SMES is used for short duration storage such as improving <a href="/wiki/Power_quality" class="mw-redirect" title="Power quality">power quality</a>. It also has applications in grid balancing.<sup id="cite_ref-Hassenzahl_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hassenzahl-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interseasonal_thermal_storage">Interseasonal thermal storage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Interseasonal thermal storage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Seasonal_thermal_energy_storage" title="Seasonal thermal energy storage">Seasonal thermal energy storage</a> (STES) allows heat or cold to be used months after it was collected from waste energy or natural sources. The material can be stored in contained aquifers, clusters of boreholes in geological substrates such as sand or crystalline bedrock, in lined pits filled with gravel and water, or water-filled mines.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mills">Mills</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Mills">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A more recent application is the control of waterways to drive water mills for processing grain or powering machinery. Complex systems of <a href="/wiki/Reservoir" title="Reservoir">reservoirs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dam" title="Dam">dams</a> were constructed to store and release water (and the <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">potential energy</a> it contained) when required.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Home_energy_storage">Home energy storage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Home energy storage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Home_Energy_Storage" title="Home Energy Storage">Home Energy Storage</a></div>
<p>Home energy storage is expected to become increasingly present given the growing importance of distributed generation (especially photovoltaics) and the important share of energy consumption in buildings.<sup id="cite_ref-Silva-Hendrick_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Silva-Hendrick-60">[60]</a></sup> A household equipped with photovoltaics can achieve a maximum electricity self-sufficiency of about 40%. To reach higher levels of self-sufficiency, energy storage is needed, given the mismatch between energy consumption and energy production from photovoltaics.<sup id="cite_ref-Silva-Hendrick_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Silva-Hendrick-60">[60]</a></sup> In 2015, multiple manufacturers announced rechargeable battery systems for storing energy, generally to hold surplus energy from home solar/wind generation.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tesla_Motors" class="mw-redirect" title="Tesla Motors">Tesla Motors</a> announced the first two models of the <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Powerwall" title="Tesla Powerwall">Tesla Powerwall</a>. One is a 10 kWh weekly cycle version for backup applications and the other is a 7 kWh version for daily cycle applications.<sup id="cite_ref-businessinsider_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-businessinsider-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Enphase_Energy" title="Enphase Energy">Enphase Energy</a> announced an integrated system that allows home users to store, monitor and manage electricity. The system stores 1.2 kWh hours of energy and 275W/500W power output.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grid_electricity">Grid electricity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Grid electricity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Grid_energy_storage" title="Grid energy storage">Grid energy storage</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Renewable_energy_storage">Renewable energy storage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_storage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Renewable energy storage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Abengoa_Solar_(7336087392).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Abengoa_Solar_%287336087392%29.jpg/220px-Abengoa_Solar_%287336087392%29.jpg" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Abengoa_Solar_%287336087392%29.jpg/330px-Abengoa_Solar_%287336087392%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Abengoa_Solar_%287336087392%29.jpg/440px-Abengoa_Solar_%287336087392%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3926" data-file-height="2495" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Abengoa_Solar_(7336087392).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Construction of the Salt Tanks which provide efficient <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy_storage" title="Thermal energy storage">thermal energy storage</a><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> so that output can be provided after the sun goes down, and output can be scheduled to meet demand requirements.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> The 280 MW <a href="/wiki/Solana_Generating_Station" title="Solana Generating Station">Solana Generating Station</a> is designed to provide six hours of energy storage. This allows the plant to generate about 38 percent of its rated capacity over the course of a year.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:12-05-08_AS1.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/12-05-08_AS1.JPG/220px-12-05-08_AS1.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/12-05-08_AS1.JPG/330px-12-05-08_AS1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/12-05-08_AS1.JPG/440px-12-05-08_AS1.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3504" data-file-height="2336" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:12-05-08_AS1.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The 150 MW <a href="/wiki/Andasol_solar_power_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Andasol solar power station">Andasol solar power station</a> is a commercial <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_trough" title="Parabolic trough">parabolic trough</a> <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar thermal">solar thermal</a> power plant, located in <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_in_Spain" title="Renewable energy in Spain">Spain</a>. The Andasol plant uses <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy_storage#Molten_salt_technology" title="Thermal energy storage">tanks of molten salt</a> to store solar energy so that it can continue generating electricity even when the sun isn't shining.<sup id="cite_ref-Cartlidge_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cartlidge-66">[66]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The largest source and the greatest store of renewable energy is provided by hydroelectric dams. A large reservoir behind a dam can store enough water to average the annual flow of a river between dry and wet seasons. A very large reservoir can store enough water to average the flow of a river between dry and wet years. While a hydroelectric dam does not directly store energy from intermittent sources, it does balance the grid by lowering its output and retaining its water when power is generated by solar or wind. If wind or solar generation exceeds the regions hydroelectric capacity, then some additional source of energy will be needed.</p>
<p>Many <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> sources (notably solar and wind) produce <a href="/wiki/Variable_renewable_energy" title="Variable renewable energy">variable power</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2010.07.28_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2010.07.28-67">[67]</a></sup> Storage systems can level out the imbalances between supply and demand that this causes. Electricity must be used as it is generated or converted immediately into storable forms.<sup id="cite_ref-Ingebretsen-Johansen_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ingebretsen-Johansen-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>The main method of electrical grid storage is <a href="/wiki/Pumped-storage_hydroelectricity" title="Pumped-storage hydroelectricity">pumped-storage hydroelectricity</a>. Areas of the world such as Norway, Wales, Japan and the US have used elevated geographic features for <a href="/wiki/Reservoir" title="Reservoir">reservoirs</a>, using electrically powered pumps to fill them. When needed, the water passes through generators and converts the gravitational potential of the falling water into electricity.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2010.07.28_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2010.07.28-67">[67]</a></sup> Pumped storage in Norway, which gets almost all its electricity from hydro, has an instantaneous capacity of 25–30 GW expandable to 60 GW—enough to be "Europe's battery".<sup id="cite_ref-Ingebretsen-Johansen_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ingebretsen-Johansen-68">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Deutsche_Welle-2014.07.21_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deutsche_Welle-2014.07.21-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some forms of storage that produce electricity include pumped-storage <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectric_dams" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydroelectric dams">hydroelectric dams</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rechargeable_battery" title="Rechargeable battery">rechargeable batteries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy_storage" title="Thermal energy storage">thermal storage</a> including <a href="/wiki/Molten_salt_heat_storage" class="mw-redirect" title="Molten salt heat storage">molten salts</a> which can efficiently store and release very large quantities of heat energy,<sup id="cite_ref-NYTimes-2014.04.21_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-2014.04.21-70">[70]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Compressed_air_energy_storage" title="Compressed air energy storage">compressed air energy storage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flywheel_energy_storage" title="Flywheel energy storage">flywheels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cryogenic_energy_storage" title="Cryogenic energy storage">cryogenic systems</a> and <a href="/wiki/Superconducting_magnetic_energy_storage" title="Superconducting magnetic energy storage">superconducting magnetic coils</a>.</p>
<p>Surplus power can also be converted into <a href="/wiki/Power_to_gas" title="Power to gas">methane</a> (<a href="/wiki/Sabatier_process" class="mw-redirect" title="Sabatier process">sabatier process</a>) with stockage in the natural gas network.<sup id="cite_ref-Schmid_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schmid-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-N.C3.A9gaWatt_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-N.C3.A9gaWatt-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
